## Page 9

REGISTRAR GENERAL'S FOREWORD
INTRODUCTION
This is the preliminary report on the seventh census of the population of Northern Ireland, taken at midnight
on 5 April 1981. The figures contained in it are provisional and relate to the population and number of private
households for each local government district. They have been compiled from the enumerators' record books,
population summaries and from the completed census forms and are subject to revision when the returns are
examined further for the later and more detailed census reports.
Censuses were also taken on the same date in other parts of the United Kingdom and the Republic of Ireland.

    Previous censuses in Northern Ireland were taken in 1926, 1937, 1951, 1961, 1966 (interim) and 1971.
The 1981 Census continues the decennial pattern of full censuses.
On this occasion considerable effort had been put into streamlining census forms as well as the range of

    questions. The 1981 census form contained 22 questions which was 6 fewer than in the 1971 census although
the basic questions remained much the same. Among the topics omitted this time were usual address 5 years
ago, qualifications at ordinary level, hours per week worked, occupation one year ago and details on fertiUty.
On the other hand the 1981 census form contained additional sub-sections about household amenities.
ORGANISATION
TheT981 census was organised on broadly similar lines to the 1971 census. The country was again divided into
small areas termed enumeration districts. This time there were about 3,000 such districts as compared with

    2,200 in 1971. Each district contained on average about 170 households. About 2,900 enumerators were
responsible for the distribution and collection of forms in these districts.
The enumeration districts were grouped into census districts, each containing on average about 30
enumeration districts. A census officer was appointed to each census district to assist in the recruitment and
training of enumerators and to oversee their work. In some cases they had responsibility for the enumeration of
certain communal estabhshments within their district. In all 100 census officers were appointed.
The third tier of the field organisation consisted of supervisors who were appointed to work from their own
homes. Each supervisor was responsible for recruiting, training and overseeing the work of about 17 census
officers.
,
Altogether therefore there were about 3,000 field staff who were recruited by Census Office through the
Employment Service Offices of the Department of Manpower Services and by means of advertising in the local
press.
TAKING THE CENSUS
The success of a population census depends greatly on the attitude of the general public and their response to
the burden of form filling. Particular thanks for their willing and understanding co-operation are due to
householders and all others responsible for the completion of forms. Thanks are also due to the BBC, ITV,
Downtown Radio and the Press for their invaluable assistance in publicising and arousing interest in the
census and also to Post Offices, business firms, local authorities, public services, schools and others for
displaying census posters on their premises.
The part played by field staff was of course vital and special mention and thanks are due to them for their
devotion in the performance of their duties, often under very difficult circumstances. A campaign of
non co-operation, which included the public burning of census forms, was conducted in some areas.
In some instances there was deplorable intimidation of enumerators culminating in the murder of one of our
enumerators while collecting census forms, in Londonderry. This crime met with widespread public revulsion
and condemnation.
Arising from difficulties in the field, special arrangements had to be devised to ensure that forms which had
been collected from the public by field staff were in the custody of the Census Office as quickly as possible.
The public were also asked to co-operate by sending in forms which had remained uncollected. Once again the
response was excellent in that initially many forms were received by post in the Census Office from households
whose enumerators had been unable to collect the completed forms. In due course, as the result of further
approaches, the number of households enumerated rose to 455,279. This represents 98.7% of the estimated
total number of households.
THE FUTURE PROGRAMME
As indicated, the figures in this report are provisional. The main census reports will be based on a carefully
edited computer analysis of the data obtained from the census forms. The task of processing the results is well
advanced and it is planned to publish the Belfast report, which is the first of the detailed reports, towards the
vii


---

## Page 10

end of this year. It will contain figures on area, population, age, marital status, religion, economic activity and
housing. It will be followed by a Summary report in early 1983 giving similar information for each Northern
Ireland local government district. The remaining detailed reports on the particular topics of economic activity,
housing, workplace, migration, education and religion will be published as soon as possible thereafter.
R. McMURRAY
Registrar General
July 1982
vm

## Page 11

1981 CENSUS PRELIMINARY RESULTS
The figures, which are provisional, are based on the actual population present and enumerated in Northern
Ireland on Census night including civilians and Service personnel enumerated on land, in vessels, on inland
waters or in ports or at anchorages and persons on ships on coastwise voyages to Northern Ireland.
As indicated under the COVERAGE heading below, the figures represent 98.7% of the total estimated
number of households. In order to be as informative as possible, additional columns have been incorporated
in the tables to indicate the estimated population effect for households not included in the enumeration,
which represent 1.3% of estimated total households.
The figures show that the population of Northern Ireland as enumerated at midnight on 5 April 1981 totalled

    1,490,228 comprising 730,174 males and 760,054 females. This figure however does not take account of the
effect of the non-returns on the population figures, estimated at 19,664. In the 1971 Census the total
enumerated population was 1,536,065.
COVERAGE
Results from any population census will inevitably contain some inaccuracies arising from:
(a)
Failure to identify all the living accommodation in an enumeration district.
(b) Incorrect classification of accommodation as vacant or occupants absent.
(c)
Failure to identify all the households in a building.
(d) Failure to make contact with some households.
(e)
Misunderstanding of the form by the form-filler.
However, the Northern Ireland 1981 Census had additional factors. As a result of difficulties which arose
during the enumeration stages of the exercise forms for many households remained undelivered or uncollected
at the final collection date of 9 April 1981. Although the bulk of the returns had been received in the Census
Office by the end of April 1981, returns for approximately 65,000 households remained outstanding. As a
result of further publicity and postal contact, this number had reduced to about 20,000 households by the end
of 1981. While at that stage it would have been possible to issue a preliminary report on the basis of what then
amounted to a 96% coverage it was considered that a further effort to make the Census as comprehensive and
reliable as possible should be made. Consequently further letters were sent to outstanding households
explaining to them the importance of making returns, and emphasising that it was in their own interests that
they should do so. These letters yielded an excellent response and as a result the number of households
enumerated rose to 455,279, which represents 98.7% of the estimated total number of households.
NATURAL INCREASE AND INTERCENSAL MOVEMENT
The figures for natural increase, ie excess of births over deaths, are obtained from the annual statistics of births
and deaths published in the Registrar General's Annual Reports. The natural increase of the population for
each intercensal period since birth and death registration commenced in 1864 is shown in Table *A' together
with the actual variations in population for each period and the net loss by migration for 1871-1971.
There have been significant changes in the population in the intercensal period between 1971 and 1981. The
number of births registered (274,786) during the period shows a decrease of 56,409 compared with the number
of births registered in the two 5 year intercensal periods between 1961 and 1971 while the number of deaths
registered (167,232) in the same comparative periods shows an increase of 9,599. The excess of births over

    deaths (107,554) in the intercensal period 1971 to 1981 when placed against the total enumerated population at
those respective dates suggest a net outward migration of around 150,000 or an average of 15,000 persons
per year over the period. If the estimated population effect of the non-response (almost 20,000) for 1981
is taken into account the net outward migration would be of the order of 130,000 or 13,000 persons

    per year. This compares with about 60,000 or 6,000 persons per year between 1961 and 1971 and about
    90,000 or 9,000 persons per year between 1951 and 1961.
The latest available Government estimate in June 1980 indicated a net outward migration of around 10,000
persons per year. The variation reflects the great difficulty, common to other countries, of obtaining
reliable information about migration. In the case of Northern Ireland the difficulty is greater because of the
existence of a land border with the RepubHc of Ireland, where population movement cannot be effectively
monitored. It is therefore inevitable that in estimating population, differences of the above nature occur.

Table 'B* below shows the natural increase, ie excess of births over deaths, for each local government district in
Northern Ireland, between 1971 and 1981, as well as the total population (enumerated, and estimated) at the
end of the period. It reveals that the population of the Belfast local government district continues to decline.

    In 1971 it was 416,679. At the Census date it was enumerated at 297,862, but the estimated effect of the'
non-response is 7,900. Taking the latter into account the reduction oveV the period is of the order of 111,000.
Belfast, as in other cities of the United Kingdom, and indeed Europe, has suffered from the problem of inner
city decline, although perhaps in a more pronounced way between 1971 and 1981. Factors such as the effects oi
the troubles, high outward migration, and redevelopment are likely to have been the major influences in the
population reduction. ^It is also relevant to note that Belfast has had a relatively low natural increase in
population (4,959), as compared for example with 14,037 for Londonderry, over the period.
.

## Page 13

The decline in the Belfast population is counterbalanced to some extent by gains in surrounding areas. The
most significant gains occur in Lisburn, North Down, Antrim, Ards and Newtownabbey which taking account

    of the population effect of non-enumerated households show gains of the order of 13,000, 13,000, 12,000,
    11,000 and 5,000 respectively. Castlereagh, with a drop of around 3,000 is the only other district showing a
significant decrease.
SEXES

    At the 1981 Census there were 730,174 males and 760,054 females enumerated. This represents a
female/male ratio of 1,041 females per 1,000 males in the enumerated population for Northern Ireland as a

    whole compared with the ratio of 1,035 at the 1971 Census and 1,051 at the 1966 Census.

TOWNS
The 1971 Preliminary Report contained population information on towns and villages consisting of clusters of
50 or more houses which were located in the administrative rural areas and which had no legally defined
boundaries. Similar information is not included in this Report, but will be made available in the more detailed
Summary Report to be published in due course following completion of the main processing of results.
In 1971 towns with legally defined boundaries could be identified as such because of their relationship to local
government administrative areas; With the introduction of the existing local government districts on
1 October 1973 under the Local Government Act (Northern Ireland) 1972, towns with legally defined
boundaries disappeared. However, it is proposed that information for towns will be available in the published
Summary Report. This will be achieved by the combination of grid squares.
PRIVATE HOUSEHOLDS
The 1981 figure for, enumerated private households is 455,279. The estimated number of households not
enumerated is 6,126. Table 3 shows the overall Northern Ireland position by each local government district and
Tuble 5 gives similar information to ward level. On the enumerated figures, the number of households had

    increased by 27,845 iathe intercensal period 1971 to 1981. The estimated number of households not reflected in
the enumerated figures is 6,126. The main increases occur in North Down, Lisburn, Ards, Antrim,
Newtownabbey, which taking into account the estimate of non-enumerated households, show increases of

    the order of 5,600, 5,300, 4,400, 4,000 and 3,900 respectively. All other local government districts, except
Belfast show an increase in the actual number of households as enumerated.
xn


---

## Page 15

TABLES 1-5
These tables provide details of the Northern Ireland population from 1821 to 1981 (Table 1), and the
population for 1971 and 1981, by local government districts and wards (Tables 2 and 4). They also provide
details of private households for 1971 and 1981 by local government districts and wards (Tables 3 and 5).
.1X1 addition they contain estimates of non-enumerated households and their effect on the population figures.
It should be noted that the estimate for a particular unit (eg the Northern Ireland total) may not equal the sum
of the estimates of component sub-units (eg local government districts). For Northern Ireland the estimate is
based on the overall enumeration and for the district the enumeration for the district. As both average
household sizes and the proportion of non-enumerated households vary from district to district, the total
achieved by aggregating districts will not necessarily be the same as the total at Northern Ireland level.
In calculating the average household size the population of major communal establishments and institutions
has been excluded.
The explanation in the preceding paragraph also applies to the estimated population figures shown in
tables B and E (see pages x and xii respectively).
xni

## Page 42

The Summary Report—Synopsis of Key Results
The subjects covered in this Report include area, population, households, age, sex, marital status, birthplace,
religion, dwelling type, tenure, household size, occupational density, amenities, cars and economically active
persons.
The tables contain information for each of the 26 local government districts. Tables 1-4 (as in tables 1-5 of
the Preliminary Report and tables 1-4 of the Belfast Report) contain two categories of figures. The first relates to
the enumerated households and these should be treated as accurate in regard to the information they contain.
The second relates to the estimated population effect of non-enumerated households which is based on
information derived from the Census results for the particular area (i.e. Northern Ireland, district or ward). Since
these are estimated figures, they must not be regarded as having the same degree of accuracy as the enumerated
figures. As with all estimates, they are subject to revision in the light of information from other sources.
All other tables refer to the enumerated households only.
Population Present on Census Night
As enumerated at midnight on 5 April 1981 the Northern Ireland population was 1,488,077. This is a

    decrease of 2,151 on the provisional figure of 1,490,228 stated in the Preliminary Report issued in July 1982. This
does not take into account the population effect of the non-returns which is estimated at around 19,000. In the
1971 Census the total enumerated population present on census night was 1,536,065.
Usually Resident Population
The number of people usually resident in Northern Ireland (i.e. excluding visitors but including residents
who were absent on census night) was enumerated at 1,481,959. Again this does not take into account the
population effect of the non-returns, which is estimated at around 19,000,
Young and Old
Compared with the 1971 Census the 1981 figures reveal a higher proportion of elderly persons in the

    population, with 14.5 per cent of persons of pensionable age (men aged 65 or over and women aged 60 or over),
and 4.4 per cent aged 75 years or more. In 1971,13.3 per cent of the population were of pensionable age and 3.8

    per cent were 75 years or more. In 1981, 27.9 per cent of the population were under 16 years whereas the
corresponding figure in 1971 was 31.5 per cent.
Men and Women at Work
The figures show that 62.8 per cent of men aged 16 and over were in employment (full-time or part-time) at

    some time in the week before census day (30 March to 5 April 1981). A further 14.9 per cent were out of
employment in the week before census day, i.e. seeking work, prevented by temporary sickness from seeking
work or waiting to take up a job already accepted. The remaining 22.3 per cent were economically inactive and, of
these, 26.6 per cent were full-time students representing 6.0 per cent of the overall total.
Of women aged 16 and over 36.4 per cent were in employment (full-time or part-time) at some time in the
week before census day.
An alternative method of measuring the unemployment rate is to express the numbers out of work as a
percentage of the numbers who are economically active, rather than as a percentage of the total numbers. On this
basis, the proportion of persons who were out of employment in the week before census day was 19.1 per cent for
men and 12.6 per cent for women.
Households
There were 456,348 enumerated private households, which on the basis of usually resident population
contained an average of 3.2 persons per household. In 1971 there were 427,434 households which on the basis of
population present contained an average of 3.5 persons per household.
Living Alone
The number of households in which one person usually lives alone was 85,238 or 18.7 per cent of private
households with a usual address in Northern Ireland. In 1971 the comparable figures, based on the population
present, were 63,903 or 15.0 per cent.
Overcrowding
In 10.4 per cent of households there was more than one person per room compared with 15.9 per cent in
1971.
iv


---

## Page 43

Owner Occupiers and Tenants
49.4 per cent of households were in owner-occupied accommodation and 39.3 per cent in accommodation

    rented from a local or public authority. The comparable 1971 figures are 45.9 per cent and 34.6 per cent
respectively.
Household Amenities
9.6 per cent of households lacked or were forced to share with another household the use of a fixed bath or
shower, compared with 28.2 per cent in 1971.
Availability of Cars
40.1 per cent of households had no car available for use by members of the household, compared with 46.4
per cent in 1971.
Religion
There was a high level of non-response to the voluntary question on religion. The figures show that 18.5 per
cent of the population in Northern Ireland failed to anwser it. This compares with 9.4 per cent in 1971.
•J


---

## Page 44

1981 CENSUS
Programme of Census Reports
1.
Preliminary Report
This report was published in July 1982 and contained provisional figures of the population and private
households for each of the 26 local government districts with a brief statistical commentary.
2.
Report for Belfast Local Government District
This report was published in December 1982 and related to the Local Government District of Belfast. The
tables contained information to Ward level. Subjects covered in this report included age distribution, sex, marital
status, birthplace, religion, households, tenure, amenities, occupation density, availability of cars, communal
establishments, economically active population and employment status.
3.
Summary
The Summary Report contains information for Northern Ireland and for each of the 26 local government
districts. The subjects covered include age distribution, sex, marital status, birthplace, religion, households,
tenure, amenities, occupation density, availability of cars, communal establishments, economically active
population and employment status.
4.
Economic Activity
This report will contain tables giving the figures for the both economically active and inactive population. The
employed population will be analysed by industry and occupation classifications and related to age, marital
status, employment status, socio-economic groupings, area of work-place, etc. Most tables will contain informa-
tion at Northern Ireland level but certain ones will give details for local government districts.
5.
Housing and Household Composition
This report will give detailed information on households, tenure, occupation density, car availability, sex,
age, marital status and will relate household composition to social and other economic characteristics.
6.
Workplace and Transport to Work
Employed population details in relation to area of workplace/residence will be contained in this report.
Workplace movement by socio-economic group, occupation, industry, means of transport and times of journey
will also be detailed to local government district level.
7.
Migration
Numbers and characteristics of the population who changed their usual address in the year before Census day
will be contained in this report. Migrant households will be detailed by age, sex, marital status, etc. Some tables
will refer to Northern Ireland and others will be detailed to local government districts.
8.
Education
This report will give details of age, sex, occupation and industry of the population who have obtained higher
educational qualifications analysed to Northern Ireland level.
9.
Religion
Tables in this report will give, by the main religious denominations, details of the population by sex, age,
marital status, occupation, industry and other characteristics.
VI
H)


---

## Page 45

Explanatory Notes and Definitions of Terms used in the Report
Area and Administrative Divisions
The figures on areas have been supplied by the Ordnance Survey and rounded to the nearest hectare. They
include the areas of the larger rivers and lakes except for those specified at appropriate footnotes to tables. A
hectare is equivalent to 2.471 acres. The local government districts and wards for which statistics are presented in
this report are those in existence at the date of census.
Population
Census results are presented as cross tabulations, each counting a particular population, for example,
people, households, families or people in communal establishments. It is important to know exactly what is being
counted, i.e. the population base. Information on whereabouts and usual address given for each person listed on
the Census form can be used to derive the population base.
The population present on census night (present population) in an area is the count of all people recorded as
spending census night in the area regardless of whether or not this was where they usually lived; that is, all persons
recorded on the Census H Forms for whom the whereabouts on census night was given as the relevant household,
plus all persons recorded as being in communal establishments.
The usually resident population in an area is the count of those people recorded on the forms for the area as
usually living 'at this address' whether or not they are present on census night.
Shipping: Apart from houseboats (enumerated as private households), vessels were treated in a similar way
to communal establishments with a listing form and individual return forms for each person on the ship. Persons
on naval vessels in Northern Ireland waters or ports on census night were included as present in the home base
port of the ship. Generally persons on other vessels were counted as present in the area in which the form was
collected. For shipping moving between a port in Northern Ireland and a port in England, Wales, Scotland, Isle of
Man or Channel Isles arrangements were made for the return of forms to the issuing country and persons in such
vessels were counted as present in the area of issue.
Campers, Vagrants, Persons sleeping rough, etc.
The present population also includes persons not enumerated in private households or communal establish-
ments, for example, vagrants, persons sleeping rough and persons enumerated in caravans or other non-
permanent structures which were not the usual residence of any persons present on census night.
Population covered in this Report
Figures contained in this report refer to the usually resident population basis with the exception of Tables 1
and 2 which compare the population present in Northern Ireland on Census night 1981 with the corresponding
figures for 1971. In Table 16 (Communal Establishments) the figures relate to the persons present in such
establishments on census night.
Households
A household is either one person living alone or a group of persons (who may or may not be related) living at
the same address with common housekeeping. Persons staying temporarily with the household are included. A
group of people are treated as a household if there is any regular arrangement to share at least one meal a day.
Two people who share one room, although each may have separate catering arrangements, are treated as one
household. A household must have exclusive use of at least one room.
Dwellings
Dwellings are classified to include private dwelling houses, bungalows or flats with independent access to the
street or to a common staircase or hall. A count of the occupied dwellings was not included in the 1981 Census as
previous attempts were not entirely successful. This was due to difficulties in identifying buildings, other than
purpose built blocks of flats, occupied by more than one household and partly due to the low incidence of multiple
household dwellings revealed in the 1971 Census.
An occupier absent dwelling is one in which at least one household usually, or occasionally, lives but was
absent on census night.
A vacant dwelling is one which was fit or ready for habitation but was awaiting occupancy - this type of
dwelling would invariably be unfurnished. Derelict or uninhabitable dwellings are excluded.
n
vn


---

## Page 46

Non-permanent dwellings
Caravans or mobile structures and such non-permanent structures as huts, shacks, tents, etc., are treated as
dwellings only if they were some person's usual residence on census night. If such structures were vacant on
census night they were excluded.
Shared dwellings
A shared dwelling is a dwelling occupied by more than one private household.
Communal Establishments
People were enumerated in a variety of communal establishments (non-private establishments) such as
hotels, boarding schools, hospitals, prisons, religious communities, etc. The population enumerated in com-
munal establishments plus vagrants and campers is referred to as the population in non-private households. It
includes all those present in the establishment on census night and all who arrived on Monday 6 April 1981 and
who had not been included as present on a census form elsewhere. Non-resident staff/personnel on duty in the
premises on census night are not included.
Separate buildings in the grounds of an establishment for the accommodation of private households such as a
doctor's house or caretaker's cottage in the grounds of a hospital, are classed as private residences and not as part
of the communal establishment. People occupying flats or suites of rooms within the main building of an
establishment are treated as part of the main establishment and included on the 4L' form. Service families or
civilians living in married quarters of the Armed Services are treated as private households whether the quarters
were located within or outside the boundaries of the service establishment.
Annexes to communal establishment are treated as part of the main establishment if located in the same
enumeration district or if meals were taken at the main establishment even though the annexe was in a different
enumeration district.
Establishment Classification
The categories are as follows:
1. Hotels, etc.
2.
National Health Service (NHS) hospitals and homes.
(i) Psychiatric,
(ii) Other.
3.
Non-NHS hospitals and homes.
(i) Psychiatric,
(ii) Other.
4.
Homes for the old and disabled.
5.
Children's homes.
6.
Educational establishments.
7.
Places of detention.
8.
Defence establishments including naval vessels and service hospitals.
9.
Civilian ships, boats and barges.
10.
Hostels and common lodging houses.
11.
Other miscellaneous establishments.
12.
Campers and vagrants.
Residence Classification
Persons enumerated in groups 1 to 7 and 10 and 11 above are given one of the 3 following residence
classification codes:
(i) Managers and staff,
(ii) Inmates,
(hi) Relatives of managers and staff and others.
All persons present in groups 8, 9 and 12 are classed under category (ii).
Age
Age is the completed years of age at census date as derived from the date of birth question.
Pensionable Age
Pensionable age is the minimum age at which a person may receive a National Insurance Retirement
Pension—60 for women and 65 for men.
vin
\


---

## Page 47

Usual Address
In most cases the answer to the usual address question is straightforward. However, students and children
away from home during term-time were to regard their home address as their usual address. Boarders were to
decide whether they considered as their usual address, the address of the relevant household. Household heads
living away from home for part of the week were to regard their home address as their usual address.
Persons who failed to answer the relevant questions appear as part of the usually resident population in the
area in which they were enumerated and are included as part of a usually resident private household.
Visitors
Visitors are those present at the relevant household address on census night whose usual address is
elsewhere.
Country of Birth
Persons born in Northern Ireland, Wales, Scotland, England or the Republic of Ireland were asked to tick
the appropriate box. Persons born in another country were asked to tick the 'Elsewhere' box and write the
present name of the country.
Religion
It was not obligatory to answer the question on religion. The question was worded as follows:
Please state the Religion, Religious Denomination or body to which the person belongs. The term
'Protestant' should not be used alone and the denomination should be given as precisely as
possible.
Religion
The classification of religions includes a separate heading for each religion. Religions which differ only or
mainly in denominational terminology are generally grouped together and the figures for the group as a whole are
shown under the name by which the group is officially or commonly known. Thus the heading Presbyterian
includes United Presbyterian, Trinitarian and Church of Scotland. Church of Ireland is regarded as embracing
both Anglican and Episcopalian except for persons so described whose usual address was in England: in such
cases the Church of England classification was applied.
Economic Activity
The economic activity tables describe the economic characteristics of the population. The characteristics
include both those obtained directly from census questions - economic position, employment status, occupation,
industry and workplace - and others derived from combining answers to these questions - social class and
socio-economic group (from employment status and occupation) and socio-economic class (from social class and
socio-economic group).
Only specified groups were required to answer such of the economic activity questions and this is reflected in
the population to which tables refer. Thus:
(a) Tables relating to economic position include only persons aged 16 years and over.
(b) Tables relating to social class or socio-economic group or socio-economic class cover the economically active,
retired from employment and permanently sick.
(c) Tables relating to occupation cover the economically active.
(d) Tables relating to employment status, industry and workplace cover persons in employment.
Economic Position
The Classification by Economic Position is as follows:
Economically Inactive
Retired means wholly retired from employment and no longer seeking paid work. Married women
engaged on unpaid home duties are not regarded as retired but classed as other persons economically
inactive.
Students are persons in full time attendance at an educational establishment irrespective of whether or
not they have part-time or seasonal employment.
ix
c^


---

## Page 48

Other persons economically inactive includes those persons never in employment and not seeking work.
Included are persons of independent means, housewives and others engaged in unpaid domestic duties
and the permanently sick or disabled.
Economically Active
In employment means having a job during the week ended 4 April 1981. Temporary, part-time or casual
employment is included. Persons absent from their employment due to holidays, strikes, lock-outs,
short time working or temporary stoppages are regarded as in employment. Persons off work sick are
regarded as in employment if their jobs are waiting for them on return.
Out of employment means seeking work or prevented by temporary sickness from seeking work or
waiting to take up a job already accepted.
Employment Status
The classification of employment status is as follows:
Self-Employed
1. Without employees.
2.
With employees.
Employees
1.
Managers.
(a) large establishments.
(b) small establishments.
2.
Foremen and supervisors.
(a) manual.
(b) non-manual.
3.
Professional employees.
4.
Apprentices, articled pupils, formal trainees.
5.
Employees (not elsewhere classified).
Information on occupation is used to distinguish the various types of employees and information on name
and business of employer to distinguish the size of the establishment. Large establishments are those
classified by the Department of Economic Development as having 25 or more employees and small
establishments as those with fewer than 25. Details of employment status codes are contained in pages

    vii-ix of the Classification of Occupations 1980 published by HMSO under ISBN 0 11 6907282.
Rooms
Private households were asked to count the number of rooms in their accommodation, but not to count the
following:
Kitchens less than 2 metres (6 feet 6 inches) wide, bathrooms, toilets, sculleries, closets, store-rooms,
landings, halls, recesses or rooms used for business, professional or trading purposes.
NOTE: Rooms divided by curtains or portable screens count as one; those divided by a fixed or sliding
partition count as 2.
In unshared dwellings the head of the household provided information. In shared dwellings, the enumerator
was instructed to enquire of each household in the dwelling if any room was shared by another household. If any
room was shared by 2 or more households the enumerator was further instructed to check the answer to the
question and if necessary, alter it to show the extent of the sharing.
In the tables classifying households by rooms occupied, any room shared by 2 or more households is allocated
to one household to avoid the use of fractions and to ensure that the total number of rooms in the dwelling is not
altered.
Tenure
Heads of households were required to indicate under the following headings how they occupied their
accommodation:
As an owner occupier (including purchase by mortgage):
1.
Of freehold property (for which no ground rent is paid).
2.
Of leasehold property (for which ground rent is paid).
x


---

## Page 49

By renting, rent free or by lease:
3.
From a public authority (e.g. N.I. Housing Executive).
4.
From a housing association or charitable trust.
5.
Unfurnished from a private landlord, company or other organisation.
6.
Furnished from a private landlord, company or other organisation.
7.
In some other way.
If more than one form of tenure was indicated Census Office decided the most appropriate answer and in the
absence of any further information assigned it to the 'in some other way' (which included the not stated) category.
A shared dwelling was classified according to the tenure stated by the main household.
Density of Occupation
The density of a private household is the ratio of the number of persons in a private household with usual
residents to the number of rooms occupied by the private household. The following ratios of density are used:
Over 1.5 persons per room.
Over one and up to 1.5 persons per room.
Over 0.75 and up to one person per room.
0.5 and over and up to 0.75 persons per room.
Less than 0.5 persons per room.
Household Amenities
The amenities on which information was collected in respect of each private household are listed below. In
the case of fixed bath or shower and flush toilets the householder was required to indicate whether individual
amenities were used only by the household, shared with another or were not available.
(a) A fixed bath or shower permanently connected to a water supply and a waste pipe.
(b) A flush toilet (WC) with entrance inside the building.
(c) A flush toilet (WC) with entrance outside the building.
In the 1981 Census additional information on amenities was collected in respect of each private household.
Householders were required to indicate whether the household had use of the following amenities:
Water supply
1. Public supply piped into house.
2.
Public supply at a standpipe.
3.
Other.
Domestic sewage disposal
1.
Public sewer.
2.
Septic tank/cesspit.
3.
Dry closet/chemical toilet.
Central heating
Central heating is a heating system designed to heat 2 or more rooms. This includes electric storage radiators
but not moveable electrical appliances such as electric fires or convector-type heaters.
Fuel or Power
The types of fuel or power used to provide the main source of heating for the household include:
1.
Solid fuel (includes coal, anthracite, wood, turf).
2.
Electricity.
3.
Oil (includes paraffin).
4.
Mains Gas.
5.
Bottled Gas.
6.
Other means.
Insulation
The types of insulation include:
1.
Roof space insulation.
2.
Cavity wall insulation.
3.
None.
-\\
xi


---

## Page 50

Cars and Vans
The number of cars and vans normally available for use by the household including any car or van provided by
employers but excluding vans used solely for the carriage of goods.
Presentation Conventions
A dash (-) indicates that the entry is zero. A blank indicates that a particular combination of line and column
is impossible. In the presentation of rates, a rate shown as 0 indicates that the actual rate is less than one half of a
unit but is not zero.
'H' Form
For reference purposes the 'FT form used for private households in the 1981 Census is produced at the end of
this report.
Explanatory Note on Tables
Inclusion of Estimated Figures
Tables 1-4 of this report (as explained in the Preliminary Report in relation to Tables 1-5 and in the Belfast
Report in relation to Tables 1-4), give estimates of non-enumerated households and their estimated effect on the
population figures. Again it should be noted that the estimate for a particular unit (e.g. Northern Ireland total)
may not equal the sum of the estimates of component sub-units (e.g. local government district). For Northern
Ireland the estimate is based on the overall enumeration for Northern Ireland and for the district the enumeration
for the district. As both average household sizes and the proportion of non-enumerated households vary from
district to district, the total achieved by aggregating districts will not necessarily be the same as the total at
Northern Ireland level. In calculating the average household size the population of communal establishments and
institutions has been excluded.
Towns
The towns listed in Table 4A of this report are urban areas with no legally defined boundaries and with a
recorded population of 1,000 or more. In previous censuses towns were defined in terms of local government
administrative areas but with local government reorganisation on 1 October 1973 towns with legally defined
boundaries disappeared. For 1981 Census purposes therefore, information for towns has been obtained by
relating urban areas to specific Irish Grid squares.

## Page 163

IN STRICT CONFIDENCE
1981 CENSUS—NORTHERN IRELAND
H Form for Private Households
A household comprises either one person living alone or a group of persons (who may or
may not be related) living at the same address with common housekeeping. Persons
staying temporarily with the household are included.
To the Head or Joint Heads or members of the Household.
Please complete this form and have it ready for collection by the Census enumerator who will call on Monday 6
April 1981 or soon after that day. If you are not sure how to answer any of the questions, the enumerator will be
glad to help you when he or she calls. He will also need to make sure that you have filled in all these entries and
you should give him any information which he may need for this purpose.
Completion of this form is compulsory under the Census Act (Northern Ireland) 1969, and if you refuse to
complete it or give false information you may have to pay a fine of up to £10.There is no penalty for refusing
to state religious denomination as this is a voluntary question.
The legal obligation to fill in the form rests on you but each person who has to be included is required to give
you the information which you need. However, if any member of the household who is aged 16 years or over
does not wish to give you his personal information, please ask the enumerator for an individual form and
envelope. The completed individual form can then be returned in the official envelope direct to the enumerator
or to the local census officer and you need only answer questions 1, 5 and 6 on this form for that person. If the
completed individual form is given to you in the envelope, you should pass it unopened to the enumerator.
The information which you give on the form will be treated as STRICTLY CONFIDENTIAL and will be used only
for compiling statistics. No names and addresses will be fed into the computer and no information about
named individuals will be passed by the Census Office to any other Government Department or to any other
Authority or person. If anyone in the census organisation improperly discloses information which you
provide, he will be liable to prosecution. Similarly, you must not disclose any information which anyone (for
example, a visitor or boarder) gives you to enable you to complete the form.
When you have completed the form, please sign the declaration at the foot of the last page.
Census Office
Management Services Building II
Stoney Road
Stormont.
BELFAST BT4 3UP
Telephone: Belfast 760711
ERIC BOSTON
Registrar General
Where boxes are provided please answer by putting a tick in the box against the answer
which applies. For example, if the answer to the marital status question is 'single', tick box
1 thus: 1 0 single. Please use ink or ballpoint pen
FOR ENUMERATOR'S USE ONLY

## Page 164

Where boxes are provided please tick the appropriate box (Please i
1-2 Beginning with the head or joint head of the household
include on this form:
(a) each person alive at midnight on 5 April 1981 who spends the night
of 5-6 April in this household.
(b) each person who usually lives in this household but spends the
night of 5-6 April elsewhere—include those in hospital, on holiday, at
school or university even if they are being put on another census form
elsewhere.
(c) all visitors, anyone on night work or travelling overnight to arr'w/e in
your household on 6 April.
(d) a new baby still in hospital—if not yet named enter 'BABY' in
Forename column.
3. Date of birth
4. Marital Status
Please tick the box showing the present marital status. If separated but
not divorced please tick 'Married (1st marriage)' or 'Re-married' as
appropriate.
5. Relationship in Household
Please tick the box which shows the relationship of each person to the
person entered in the first column.
Please write in relationship of 'other relative'—for example, father,
daughter-in-law, brother-in-law, niece, uncle, cousin, grandchild.
Please write in position in household of 'unrelated person'—for
example, boarder, housekeeper, friend, flatmate, foster child etc.
6. Whereabouts on night of 5-6 April 1981
Please tick the appropriate box to indicate where the person was on the
night of 5-6 April 1981.
7. Usual Address
If the person usually lives here tick 'this address' box. If not then tick
elsewhere' box and enter the person's usual address.
Students and children away from home during term-time should regard
their home address as their usual address.
Boarders should decide if they consider this their usual address.
The home address should be taken as the usual address for a head of
household who lives away from home for part of the week.
8. Usual Address one year ago
If the person's usual address one year ago (i.e. on 5 April 1980) was the
same as that given in answer to question 7 please tick 'same' if not,
please tick 'different' and write in the usual address.
If everyone on the form has moved from the same address, please
write the address in full for the first person and indicate with an arrow
that this applies to the other people on the form.
For a child born since 5 April 1980 write 'UNDER ONE'.
L
9. Country of birth
If the person was born in Northern Ireland, Wales, Scotland, England or
the Republic of Ireland please tick the appropriate box. If the person
was born in another country tick box 6 'Elsewhere' and write the
present name of that country.
10. Religion
Please state the Religion, Religious Denomination or Body to which the
person belongs. The general term 'Protestant' should not be used alone
and the denomination should be given as precisely as possible.

    Note: Questions 11 to 17 do not apply to persons under 16 years of age (born after 5 April 1965)
11. Whether working, retired, housewife, etc. last week
Please tick all boxes appropriate to the person's activity last week.
A job (box 1 and box 2) means any work for pay or profit but not
unpaid work. It includes:
casual or temporary work
work on a person's own account
work in a family business
part-time work even if only for a few hours
A part-time job (box 2) is a job in which the hours worked, excluding
any overtime are usually 30 hours or less per week.
Tick box 1 or box 2, as appropriate, if the person had a job but was not
at work for all or part of the week because he or she was:
on holiday
temporarily laid off
on strike
For a full-time student tick box 9 as well as any other appropriate
boxes.
Do not count as a full-time student, a person in a paid occupation in
which training is also given, such as a student nurse, an apprentice or a
management trainee.

— please answer questions 12-16 in respect of the main job during the week.
— please answer questions 12-14 in respect of the most recent full-time job, if any.

    — please write 'Not applicable' at question 12 and leave questions 13-16 blank.
12. Name and Business of Employer (if self-employed
the name and nature of the person's business)
At (a) give the trading name if one is used and avoid abbreviations or
initials At (b) please describe clearly what the employer (or
self-employed person) makes or does.
For members of the Armed Forces, civil servants and local government
officers see note 1 on back page before answering this question.
For a person employed in private domestic service please write
'Domestic Service'.
13. Occupation
At (a) please give full and precise details of the person's occupation.
Terms such as fitter or engineer should not be used by themselves.
Greater detail is required, for example, tool-room fitter, electrical
engineer etc. If unemployed or retired state the usual occupation when
working.
At (b) describe the actual work done.
Civil Servants: At (a) civil servants should write 'Civil Servant' and at
(b) non-industrial civil servants should state their rank or grade and
industrial civil servants should give the job title only, for example, radio
mechanic or wood working machinist.
Other Public Officials: Local Government and other public officials
should give their rank or grade at (a) and complete (b).
Armed Forces: Members should give their rank or rating at (a) and
leave (b) blank.
14. Employment Status
Please tick one box only.
Members of the Armed Forces should not answer this question.

## Page 178

The Belfast Report—Summary of Key Results
In this report references to Belfast mean the local government district of Belfast. The subjects covered include
area, population, households, age, sex, marital status, birthplace, religion, dwelling type, tenure, household
size, occupational density, amenities, cars and economically active persons.
The tables contain information to ward level. Tables 1-4 (as in tables 1-5 of the Preliminary Report) contain
two categories of figures. The first relates to the enumerated households and these should be treated as accurate
in regard to the information they contain. The second relates to the estimated population effect of non-
enumerated households (see note on page xii of this Report). These are based on the figures for the particular
district or ward to which they relate. Since these are estimated figures, they must not be regarded as having the
same degree of accuracy as the enumerated figures. As with all estimates, they are subject to revision in the light
of other information.
All other tables refer to enumerated households only.
Population Present on Census Night

    As enumerated at midnight on 5 April 1981 the Belfast population was 297,983. This is an increase of 121 on
the provisional figure of 297,862 stated in the Preliminary Report issued in July 1982. This does not take into
account the population effect of the non-returns which is estimated at around 7,700. In the 1971 Census the total
enumerated population present on census night was 416,679.
Usually Resident Population
The number of people usually resident in the Belfast district (i.e. excluding visitors but including residents
who were absent on census night) was enumerated at 295,223. Again this does not take into account the
population effect of the non-returns, which is estimated at 7,700.
Young and Old
The figures reveal a higher proportion of elderly persons in the population as compared with 1971. Of the
usually resident population in Belfast 23.1 per cent was aged under 16 years. Persons of pensionable age (i.e. men

    aged 65 or over and women aged 60 or over) accounted for 18.4 per cent. Persons aged 75 years or more
    represented 5.8 per cent of the usually resident population. In 1971,29.0 per cent were aged under 16 years, 15.0
per cent were of pensionable age and 4.0 per cent were aged 75 years or more.
Men and Women at Work
The figures show that 59.6 per cent of men aged 16 and over were in employment (full-time or part-time) at

    some time in the week before census day (30 March to 5 April 1981). A further 16.2 per cent were out of
employment in the week before census day, i.e. seeking work, prevented by temporary sickness from seeking
Avork or waiting to take up a job already accepted. The remaining 24.2 per cent were economically inactive and, of
these, 26.5 per cent were full-time students representing 6.4 per cent of the overall total.
Of women aged 16 and over 37.7 per cent were in employment (full-time or part-time) at some time in the
week before census day.
An alternative method of measuring the unemployment rate is to express the numbers out of work as a
percentage of the numbers who are economically active, rather than as a percentage of the total numbers. On this
basis, the proportion of persons who were out of employment in the week before census day was 21.4 per cent for
men and 13.5 per cent for women.
Households
There were 102,863 enumerated private households, which on the basis of usually resident population
contained an average of 2.8 persons per household. In 1971 there were 125,388 households which on the basis of
population present contained an average of 3.2 persons per household.
Living Alone
The number of households in which one person usually lives alone was 25,442 or 24.7 per cent of private
households with a usual address in Belfast. In 1971 the comparable figures, based on the population present, were
23,119 or 18.4 per cent.
Overcrowding
In 9.0 per cent of households there was more than one person per room compared with 13.9 per cent in 1971.
IV


---

## Page 179

Owner Occupiers and Tenants
50.1 per cent of households were in owner-occupied accommodation and 30.7 per cent in accommodation

    rented from a local or public authority. The comparable 1971 figures are 43.1 per cent and 25.3 per cent
respectively.
Household Amenities
16.3 per cent of households lacked or were forced to share with another household the use of a fixed bath or
shower, compared with 34.6 per cent in 1971.
Availability of Cars
57.8 per cent of households had no car available for use by members of the household, compared with 62.4
per cent in 1971.
Religion
There was a high level of non-response to the voluntary question on religion. The figures show that 20.6 per
cent of the population in Belfast failed to answer it. This compares with 10.2 per cent in 1971 for the former
Belfast County Borough.
v


---

## Page 180

1981 CENSUS
Programme of Census Reports
1. Preliminary Report
This report was published in July 1982 and contained provisional figures of the population and private
households for each of the 26 local government districts with a brief statistical commentary.
2.
Report for Belfast Local Government District
This report relates to the Local Government District of Belfast and the tables contain information to Ward
level. Subjects covered in this report include age distribution, sex, marital status, birthplace, religion, house-
holds, tenure, amenities, occupation density, availability of cars, communal establishments, economically active
population and employment status.
3.
Summary
The Summary Report will contain information for Northern Ireland and for each of the 26 local government
districts. The subjects covered will include age distribution, sex, marital status, birthplace, religion, households,
tenure, amenities, occupation density, availability of cars, communal establishments, economically active
population and employment status.
4.
Economic Activity
This report will contain tables giving the figures for the both economically active and inactive population. The
employed population will be analysed by industry and occupation classifications and related to age, marital
status, employment status, socio-economic groupings, area of work-place, etc. Most tables will contain informa-
tion at Northern Ireland level but certain ones will give details for local government districts.
5.
Housing and Household Composition
This report will give detailed information on households, tenure, occupation density, car availability, sex,
age, marital status and will relate household composition to social and other economic characteristics.
6.
Workplace and Transport to Work
Employed population details in relation to area of workplace/residence will be contained in this report.
Workplace movement by socio-economic group, occupation, industry, means of transport and times of journey
will also be detailed to local government district level.
7.
Migration
Numbers and characteristics of the population who changed their usual address in the year before Census day
will be contained in this report. Migrant households will be detailed by age, sex, marital status, etc. Some tables
will refer to Northern Ireland and others will be detailed to local government districts.
8.
Education
This report will give details of age, sex, occupation and industry of the population who have obtained higher
educational qualifications analysed to Northern Ireland level.
9.
Religion
Tables in this report will give, by the main religious denominations, details of the population by sex, age,
marital status, occupation, industry and other characteristics.
VI


---

## Page 181

Explanatory Notes and Definitions of Terms used in the Report
Area and Administrative Divisions
The figures on areas have been supplied by the Ordnance Survey and rounded to the nearest hectare. They
include the areas of the larger rivers and lakes except for those specified at appropriate footnotes to tables. A
hectare is equivalent to 2.471 acres. The 51 Wards for which statistics are presented in this report are those in
existence at the date of census.
Population
Census results are presented as cross tabulations, each counting a particular population, for example,
people, households, families or people in communal establishments. It is important to know exactly what is being
counted, i.e. the population base. Information on whereabouts and usual address given for each person listed on
the Census form can be used to derive the population base.
The population present on census night (present population) in an area is the count of all people recorded as
spending census night in the area regardless of whether or not this was where they usually lived; that is, all persons
recorded on the Census H Forms for whom the whereabouts on census night was given as the relevant household,
plus all persons recorded as being in communal establishments.
The usually resident population in an area is the count of those people recorded on the forms for the area as
usually living 'at this address' whether or not they are present on census night.
Shipping: Apart from houseboats (enumerated as private households), vessels were treated in a similar way
to communal establishments with a listing form and individual return forms for each person on the ship. Persons
on naval vessels in Northern Ireland waters or ports on census night were included as present in the home base
port of the ship. Generally persons on other vessels were counted as present in the area in which the form was
collected. For shipping moving between a port in Northern Ireland and a port in England, Wales, Scotland, Isle of
Man or Channel Isles arrangements were made for the return of forms to the issuing country and persons in such
vessels were counted as present in the area of issue.
Campers, Vagrants, Persons sleeping rough, etc.
The present population also includes persons not enumerated in private households or communal establish-
ments, for example, vagrants, persons sleeping rough and persons enumerated in caravans or other non-
permanent structures which were not the usual residence of any persons present on census night.
Population covered in this Report
Figures contained in this report refer to the usually resident population basis with the exception of Tables 1
and 2 which compare the population present in the Belfast Local Government District on Census night 1981 with
the corresponding figures for 1971. In Table 16 (Communal Establishments) the figures relate to the persons
present in such establishments on census night.
Households
A household is either one person living alone or a group of persons (who may or may not be related) living at
the same address with common housekeeping. Persons staying temporarily with the household are included. A
group of people are treated as a household if there is any regular arrangement to share at least one meal a day.
Two people who share one room, although each may have separate catering arrangements, are treated as one
household. A household must have exclusive use of at least one room.
Dwellings
Dwellings are classified to include private dwelling houses, bungalows or flats with independent access to the
street or to a common staircase or hall. A count of the occupied dwellings was not included in the 1981 Census as
previous attempts were not entirely successful. This was due to difficulties in identifying buildings, other than
purpose built blocks of flats, occupied by more than one household and partly due to the low incidence of multiple
household dwellings revealed in the 1971 Census.
An occupier absent dwelling is one in which at least one household usually, or occasionally, lives but was
absent on census night.
A vacant dwelling is one which was fit or ready for habitation but was awaiting occupancy - this type of
dwelling would invariably be unfurnished. Derelict or uninhabitable dwellings are excluded.
vn


---

## Page 182

Non-permanent dwellings
Caravans or mobile structures and such non-permanent structures as huts, shacks, tents, etc., are treated as
dwellings only if they were some person's usual residence on census night. If such structures were vacant on
census night they were excluded.
Shared dwellings
A shared dwelling is a dwelling occupied by more than one private household.
Communal Establishments
People were enumerated in a variety of communal establishments (non-private establishments) such as
hotels, boarding schools, hospitals, prisons, religious communities, etc. The population enumerated in com-
munal establishments plus vagrants and campers is referred to as the population in non-private households. It
includes all those present in the establishment on census night and all who arrived on Monday 6 April 1981 and
who had not been included as present on a census form elsewhere. Non-resident staff/personnel on duty in the
premises on census night are not included.
Separate buildings in the grounds of an establishment for the accommodation of private households such as a
doctor's house or caretaker's cottage in the grounds of a hospital, are classed as private residences and not as part
of the communal establishment. People occupying flats or suites of rooms within the main building of an
establishment are treated as part of the main establishment and included on the 'L' form. Service families or
civilians living in married quarters of the Armed Services are treated as private households whether the quarters
were located within or outside the boundaries of the service establishment.
Annexes to communal establishment are treated as part of the main establishment if located in the same
enumeration district or if meals were taken at the main establishment even though the annexe was in a different
enumeration district.
Establishment Classification
The categories are as follows:
1.
Hotels, etc.
2.
National Health Service (NHS) hospitals and homes.
(i) Psychiatric,
(ii) Other.
3.
Non-NHS hospitals and homes.
(i) Psychiatric,
(ii) Other.
4.
Homes for the Old and the disabled.
5.
Children's homes.
6.
Educational establishments.
7.
Places of detention.
8.
Defence establishments including Naval Vessels and Service hospitals.
9.
Civilian Ships, Boats and Barges.
10.
Hostels and common lodging houses.
11.
Other miscellaneous establishments.
12.
Campers and vagrants.
Residence Classification
Persons enumerated in groups 1 to 7 and 10 and 11 above are given one of the 3 following residence
classification codes:
(i) Managers and staff,
(ii) Inmates,
(iii) Relatives of managers and staff and others.
All persons present in groups 8, 9 and 12 are classed under category (ii).
Age
Age is the completed years of age at census date as derived from the date of birth question.
Pensionable Age
Pensionable age is the minimum age at which a person may receive a National Insurance Retirement
Pension—60 for women and 65 for men.
vm


---

## Page 183

Usual Address
In most cases the answer to the usual address question is straightforward. However, students and children
away from home during term-time were to regard their home address as their usual address. Boarders were to
decide whether they considered as their usual address, the address of the relevant household. Household heads
living away from home for part of the week were to regard their home address as their usual address.
Persons who failed to answer the relevant questions appear as part of the usually resident population in the
area in which they were enumerated and are included as part of a usually resident private household.
Visitors
Visitors are those present at the relevant household address on census night whose usual address is
elsewhere.
Country of Birth
Persons born in Northern Ireland, Wales, Scotland, England or the Republic of Ireland were asked to tick
the appropriate box. Persons born in another country were asked to tick the 'Elsewhere' box and write the
present name of the country.
Religion
It was not obligatory to answer the question on religion. The question was worded as follows:
Please state the Religion, Religious Denomination or body to which the person belongs. The term
'Protestant' should not be used alone and the denomination should be given as precisely as
possible.
Religion
The classification of religions includes a separate heading for each religion. Religions which differ only or
mainly in denominational terminology are generally grouped together and the figures for the group as a whole are
shown under the name by which the group is officially or commonly known. Thus the heading Presbyterian
includes United Presbyterian, Trinitarian and Church of Scotland. Church of Ireland is regarded as embracing
both Anglican and Episcopalian except for persons so described whose usual address was in England: in such
cases the Church of England classification was applied.
Economic Activity
The economic activity tables describe the economic characteristics of the population. The characteristics
include both those obtained directly from census questions - economic position, employment status, occupation,
industry and workplace - and others derived from combining answers to these questions - social class and
socio-economic group (from employment status and occupation) and socio-economic class (from social class and
socio-economic group).
Only specified groups were required to answer such of the economic activity questions and this is reflected in
the population to which tables refer. Thus:
(a) Tables relating to economic position include only persons aged 16 years and over.
(b) Tables relating to social class or socio-economic group or socio-economic class cover the economically active,
retired from employment and permanently sick.
(c) Tables relating to occupation cover the economically active.
(d) Tables relating to employment status, industry and workplace cover persons in employment.
Economic Position
The Classification by Economic Position is as follows:
Economically Inactive
Retired means wholly retired from employment and no longer seeking paid work. Married women
engaged on unpaid home duties are not regarded as retired but classed as other persons economically
inactive.
Students are persons in full time attendance at an educational establishment irrespective of whether or
not they have part-time or seasonal employment.
IX


---

## Page 184

Other persons economically inactive includes those persons never in employment and not seeking work.
Included are persons of independent means, housewives and others engaged in unpaid domestic duties
and the permanently sick or disabled.
Economically Active
In employment means having a job during the week ended 4 April 1981. Temporary, part-time or casual
employment is included. Persons absent from their employment due to holidays, strikes, lock-outs,
short time working or temporary stoppages are regarded as in employment. Persons off work sick are
regarded as in employment if their jobs are waiting for them on return.
Out of employment means seeking work or prevented by temporary sickness from seeking work or
waiting to take up a job already accepted.
Employment Status
The classification of employment status is as follows:
Self-Employed
1. Without employees.
2. With employees.
Employees
1. Managers.
(a) large establishments.
(b) small establishments.
2. Foremen and supervisors.
(a) manual.
(b) non-manual.
3. Professional employees.
4. Apprentices, articled pupils, formal trainees.
5. Employees (not elsewhere classified).
Information on occupation is used to distinguish the various types of employees and information on name
and business of employer to distinguish the size of the establishment. Large establishments are those
classified by the Department of Economic Development as having 25 or more employees and small
establishments as those with fewer than 25. Details of employment status codes are contained in pages
vii-ix of the Classification of Occupations 1980 published by HMSO under ISBN Oil 6907282.
Rooms
Private households were asked to count the number of rooms in their accommodation, but not to count the
following:
Kitchens less than 2 metres (6 feet 6 inches) wide, bathrooms, toilets, sculleries, closets, store-rooms,
landings, halls, recesses or rooms used for business, professional or trading purposes.
NOTE: Rooms divided by curtains or portable screens count as one; those divided by a fixed or sliding
partition count as 2.
In unshared dwellings the head of the household provided information. In shared dwellings, the enumerator
was instructed to enquire of each household in the dwelling if any room was shared by another household. If any
room was shared by 2 or more households the enumerator was further instructed to check the answer to the
question and if necessary, alter it to show the extent of the sharing.
In the tables classifying households by rooms occupied, any room shared by 2 or more households is allocated
to one household to avoid the use of fractions-and to ensure that the total number of rooms in the dwelling is not
altered.
Tenure
Heads of households were required to indicate under the following headings how they occupied their
accommodation:
As an owner occupier (including purchase by mortgage):
1. Of freehold property (for which no ground rent is paid).
2. Of leasehold property (for which ground rent is paid).
x


---

## Page 185

By renting, rent free or by lease:
3.
From a public authority (e.g. N.I. Housing Executive).
4.
From a housing association or charitable trust.
5.
Unfurnished from a private landlord, company or other organisation.
6.
Furnished from a private landlord, company or other organisation.
7.
In some other way.
If more than one form of tenure was indicated Census Office decided the most appropriate answer and in the
absence of any further information assigned it to the 'in some other way' (which included the not stated) category.
A shared dwelling was classified according to the tenure stated by the main household.
Density of Occupation
The density of a private household is the ratio of the number of persons in a private household with usual
residents to the number of rooms occupied by the private household. The following ratios of density are used:
Over 1.5 persons per room.
Over one and up to 1.5 persons per room.
Over 0.75 and up to one person per room.
0.5 and over and up to 0.75 persons per room.
Less than 0.5 persons per room.
Household Amenities
The amenities on which information was collected in respect of each private household are listed below. In
the case of fixed bath or shower and flush toilets the householder was required to indicate whether individual
amenities were used only by the household, shared with another or were not available.
(a) A fixed bath or shower permanently connected to a water supply and a waste pipe.
(b) A flush toilet (WC) with entrance inside the building.
(c) A flush toilet (WC) with entrance outside the building.
In the 1981 Census additional information on amenities was collected in respect of each private household.
Householders were required to indicate whether the household had use of the following amenities:
Water supply
1.
Public supply piped into house.
2.
Public supply at a standpipe.
3.
Other.
Domestic sewage disposal
1. Public sewer.
2.
Septic tank/cesspit.
3.
Dry closet/chemical toilet.
Central heating
Central heating is a heating system designed to heat 2 or more rooms. This includes electric storage radiators
but not moveable electrical appliances such as electric fires or convector-type heaters.
Fuel or Power
The types of fuel or power used to provide the main source of heating for the household include:
1.
Solid fuel (includes coal, anthracite, wood, turf).
2.
Electricity.
3.
Oil (includes paraffin).
4.
Mains Gas.
5.
Bottled Gas.
6.
Other means.
Insulation
The types of insulation include:
1.
Roof space insulation.
2.
Cavity wall insulation.
3.
None.
XI


---

## Page 186

Cars and Vans
The number of cars and vans normally available for use by the household including any car or van provided by
employers but excluding vans used solely for the carriage of goods.
Presentation Conventions
A dash (-) indicates that the entry is zero. A blank indicates that a particular combination of line and column
is impossible. In the presentation of rates, a rate shown as 0 indicates that the actual rate is less than one half of a
unit but is not zero.
<H' Form
For reference purposes the 'H' form used for private households in the 1981 Census is produced at the end of
this report.
Explanatory Note on Tables 1-4
Tables 1-4 of this report (as explained in the Preliminary Report in relation to Tables 1-5), gives estimates of
non-enumerated households and their estimated effect on the population figures. Again it should be noted that
the estimate for a particular unit (e.g. the Belfast local government district total) may not equal the sum of the
estimates of component sub-units (e.g. wards). For Belfast the estimate is based on the overall enumeration for
the local government district and for the ward the enumeration for the ward. As both average household sizes and
the proportion of non-enumerated households vary from ward to ward, the total achieved by aggregating wards
will not necessarily be the same as the total at Belfast local government district level. In calculating the average
household size the population of communal establishments and institutions has been excluded.

## Page 371

IN STRICT CONFIDENCE
1981 CENSUS—NORTHERN IRELAND
H Form for Private Households
A household comprises either one person living alone or a group of persons (who may or
may not be related) living at the same address with common housekeeping. Persons
staying temporarily with the household are included.
To the Head or Joint Heads or members of the Household.
Please complete this form and have it ready for collection by the Census enumerator who will call on Monday 6
April 1981 or soon after that day. If you are not sure how to answer any of the questions, the enumerator will be
glad to help you when he or she calls. He will also need to make sure that you have filled in all these entries and
you should give him any information which he may need for this purpose.
Completion of this form is compulsory under the Census Act (Northern Ireland) 1969, and if you refuse to
complete it or give false information you may have to pay a fine of up to £10. There is no penalty for refusing
to state religious denomination as this is a voluntary question.
The legal obligation to fill in the form rests on you but each person who has to be included is required to give
you the information which you need. However, if any member of the household who is aged 16 years or over
does not wish to give you his personal information, please ask the enumerator for an individual form and
envelope. The completed individual form can then be returned in the official envelope direct to the enumerator
or to the local census officer and you need only answer questions 1, 5 and 6 on this form for that person. If the
completed individual form is given to you in the envelope, you should pass it unopened to the enumerator.
The information which you give on the form will be treated as STRICTLY CONFIDENTIAL and will be used only
for compiling statistics. No names and addresses will be fed into the computer and no information about
named individuals will be passed by the Census Office to any other Government Department or to any other
Authority or person. If anyone in the census organisation improperly discloses information which you
provide, he will be liable to prosecution. Similarly, you must not disclose any information which anyone (for
example, a visitor or boarder) gives you to enable you to complete the form.
When you have completed the form, please sign the declaration at the foot of the last page.
Census Office
Management Services Building II
Stoney Road
Stormont.
BELFAST BT4 3UP
Telephone: Belfast 760711
ERIC BOSTON
Registrar General
Where boxes are provided please answer by putting a tick in the box against the answer
which applies. For example, if the answer to the marital status question is 'single', tick box
1 thus: 1 0 single. Please use ink or ballpoint pen
FOR ENUMERATORS USE ONLY
E.D. No.

Form No.

Town or Village
Street etc. with No. or name of house

Females
